{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01HZzrGYFBWucugWoDTtyoL8","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# Effect of the VKORC1 D36Y variant on warfarin dose requirement and pharmacogenetic dose prediction\n     2→\n     3→## Metadata\n     4→**Authors:** D Kurnik, H Qasim, S Sominsky, A Lubetsky, N Markovits, C Li, C M Stein, H Halkin, E Gak, R Loebstein\n     5→**Journal:** Thrombosis and haemostasis\n     6→**Date:** 2012 Aug 7\n     7→**DOI:** [10.1160/TH12-03-0151](https://doi.org/10.1160/TH12-03-0151)\n     8→**PMID:** 22871975\n     9→**PMCID:** PMC3461592\n    10→**URL:** https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3461592/\n    11→**PDF:** [https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC3461592/pdf/nihms400790.pdf](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC3461592/pdf/nihms400790.pdf)\n    12→\n    13→## Abstract\n    14→\n    15→Pharmacogenetic dosing algorithms help predict warfarin maintenance doses, but their predictive performance differs in different populations, possibly due to unsuspected population-specific genetic variants. The objectives of this study were to quantify the effect of the VKORC1 D36Y variant (a marker of warfarin resistance previously described in 4% of Ashkenazi Jews) on warfarin maintenance doses and to examine how this variant affects the performance of the International Warfarin Pharmacogenetic Consortium (IWPC) dose prediction model. In 210 Israeli patients on chronic warfarin therapy recruited at a tertiary care center, we applied the IWPC model and then added D36Y genotype as covariate to the model (IWPC+D36Y) and compared predicted with actual doses. Median weekly warfarin dose was 35 mg (interquartile range [IQR], 24.5 to 52.5 mg). Among 16 heterozygous D36Y carriers (minor allele frequency = 3.8%), warfarin weekly dose was increased by a median of 43.7 mg (IQR, 40.5 to 47.2 mg) compared to non-carriers after adjustment for all IWPC parameters, a greater than 2-fold dose increase. The IWPC model performed suboptimally (coefficient of determination R2=27.0%; mean absolute error (MAE), 14.4 ± 16.2 mg/week). Accounting for D36Y genotype using the IWPC+D36Y model resulted in a significantly better model performance (R2=47.2%, MAE=12.6±12.4 mg/week). In conclusion, even at low frequencies, variants with a strong impact on warfarin dose may greatly decrease the performance of a commonly used dose prediction model. Unexpected discrepancies of the performance of universal prediction models in subpopulations should prompt searching for unsuspected confounders, including rare genetic variants.\n    16→\n    17→Keywords: Warfarin, pharmacogenetics, VKORC1, dose prediction, ethnicity\n    18→\n    19→## Introduction\n    20→\n    21→Initiation of warfarin therapy is notoriously difficult, mainly because of the large (>20-fold) interindividual variability in dose requirements, the narrow therapeutic index, and the substantial morbidity and mortality associated with lack of therapeutic control ([1](#R1)). Genetic variants in CYP2C9, the enzyme that metabolizes warfarin, and VKORC1, the drug target for coumarins, strongly affect sensitivity to warfarin and therefore the dose of warfarin required for stable anticoagulation ([2](#R2)). Thus, dosing algorithms that include information about genetic variants have been developed and validated to improve prediction of an individual patient’s warfarin dose ([3](#R3)–[6](#R6)). These algorithms greatly improve the accuracy of the predicted stable warfarin dose compared to approaches that use fixed doses or doses determined by clinical determinants only (such as age, height, weight, and concomitant medications), and thus may reduce fluctuations of anticoagulation control during warfarin initiation and possibly the considerable attendant morbidity and mortality ([7](#R7)–[10](#R10)). The efficacy and cost-effectiveness of using pharmacogenetic information to guide the selection of warfarin doses at initiation are currently being examined in large multicenter studies ([11](#R11)); nevertheless, physicians have been encouraged to use genetic information, if available, to better predict warfarin dose in individual patients ([2](#R2), [12](#R12)).\n    22→\n    23→Prediction algorithms derived in a specific population are often less informative in populations of different ancestry ([13](#R13)–[15](#R15)). Racial differences in warfarin sensitivity are well-recognized ([16](#R16)), and much of this variability is explained by differences among races in the prevalence of common CYP2C9 and VKORC1 variants used to predict warfarin doses ([3](#R3), [4](#R4), [13](#R13), [16](#R16)). Thus, multiracial and international cohorts with thousands of patients have been studied to develop pharmacogenetic dose prediction models that are globally applicable ([3](#R3), [4](#R4)). These models are more robust across multiracial populations ([15](#R15)) and are therefore widely recommended for the implementation of pharmacogenetics-based warfarin dosing ([2](#R2)).\n    24→\n    25→We recently found the previously identified ([17](#R17)) rare *VKORC1* coding variant D36Y (rs61742245), that results in the substitution of tyrosine for aspartic acid at amino acid 36, in 8% of unselected patients receiving warfarin at our institution ([18](#R18)). The D36Y variant is associated with warfarin resistance and has been identified mainly in sporadic case reports of patients with high warfarin dose requirements ([17](#R17), [19](#R19), [20](#R20)). However, due to the small sample sizes in these previous reports, the magnitude of the independent effect of D36Y on warfarin maintenance dose has not been adequately quantified. Moreover, little is known about how carriers of this variant in a given population affect the performance of warfarin dose-prediction algorithms.\n    26→\n    27→Accordingly, the two objectives of the present study were (a) to quantify the independent effect of the D36Y *VKORC1* variant on warfarin dose requirements in a cohort large enough to allow adjustment for confounders, and (b) to examine its effect on the performance of a standard pharmacogenetic dose-prediction algorithm.\n    28→\n    29→## Methods\n    30→\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. Multiple regression analysis in the Hispanic–American cohort revealed that each CYP4F2 433M allele was associated with a 22% increase in warfarin maintenance dose (p = 0.019). Possession of the NQO1*2 allele was associated with a 34% increase in warfarin maintenance dose (p = 0.004), while adjusting for associated genetic (CYP2C9, CYP4F2 and VKORC1) and clinical factors. In this population, the inclusion of CYP4F2 and NQO1*2 genotypes improved the dose variability explained by the model from 0.58 to 0.68 (p = 0.001), a 17% relative improvement. By contrast, there was no association between CYP4F2 or NQO1*2 genotype and therapeutic warfarin dose in African–Americans after adjusting for known genetic and clinical predictors.\n    23→\n    24→**Conclusion:** \n    25→In our cohort of inner-city Hispanic–Americans, the CYP4F2 and NQO1*2 genotypes significantly contributed to warfarin dose requirements. If our findings are confirmed, they would suggest that inclusion of the CYP4F2 and NQO1*2 genotypes in warfarin dose prediction algorithms may improve the predictive ability of such algorithms in Hispanic–Americans.\n    26→\n    27→Keywords: CYP2C9, CYP4F2, genotype, NQO1, VKORC1, warfarin\n    28→\n    29→### Aim\n    30→\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_011Vbrnt2J67yzPjKUzCRC1e","type":"tool_result","content":[{"type":"text","text":"I now have sufficient data from all 31 papers to compile a comprehensive summary. Let me provide the complete analysis:\n\n---\n\n## Comprehensive Analysis of rs2108622 (CYP4F2) Associations Across All 31 Papers\n\n### Variant Identity\n\nrs2108622 is a missense SNP in exon 11 of the CYP4F2 gene, causing a V433M (Val433Met, p.V433M) amino acid substitution, also denoted as CYP4F2*3, c.1297G>A (sometimes written 1347C>T in older notation). It is located on chromosome 19p13.12.\n\n---\n\n## Drugs Associated with rs2108622\n\n### Primary Drug: Warfarin\n\nWarfarin is the drug most consistently and overwhelmingly associated with rs2108622 across all 31 papers. The T (minor) allele of rs2108622 is associated with **higher warfarin dose requirements**. Carriers of the TT genotype require approximately 1 mg/day more warfarin than CC homozygotes; CT heterozygotes fall in between. The biological mechanism: CYP4F2 is a vitamin K1 (VK1) oxidase; the T allele leads to reduced hepatic CYP4F2 protein and enzyme activity, resulting in elevated hepatic VK1 levels, which in turn requires more warfarin to achieve the same anticoagulant effect.\n\n### Secondary Drugs (Coumarins/Vitamin K Antagonists)\n\nSeveral papers explicitly address other coumarin-class anticoagulants:\n\n- **Acenocoumarol** - explicitly included in meta-analyses (PMC6542461, PMC3731755). The CYP4F2*3 effect is reported to be **larger** in patients taking acenocoumarol than warfarin.\n- **Phenprocoumon** - included in the meta-analysis (PMC6542461); one cohort studied.\n- **Fluindione** - studied alongside warfarin in the pediatric VKA study (PMC3384479), though CYP4F2 was not significantly associated with fluindione dose in that study.\n- **Coumarins (generic)** - both meta-analyses (PMC3731755, PMC6542461) use \"coumarin\" as the umbrella term for all VKAs in the drug class.\n\n### Other Drugs Mentioned in Context (not directly associated with rs2108622 as the causal variant, but studied alongside it)\n\nThese appear in multi-drug pharmacogenetic studies testing CYP4F2 alongside other agents:\n- **Aspirin** (PMC2992873 - WARIS-II study: warfarin vs. aspirin vs. combination)\n- **Amiodarone** - repeatedly listed as a concomitant medication affecting warfarin dose in regression models (PMC2911553, PMC5508045, PMC5610780)\n- **Antiplatelet drugs** (PMC5508045)\n\n---\n\n## Phenotypes/Conditions Associated with rs2108622\n\n### Pharmacokinetic/Pharmacodynamic Phenotypes (Primary Outcomes)\n\n1. **Warfarin/coumarin maintenance dose requirement** - The principal phenotype across all 31 papers. The T allele is consistently associated with higher maintenance dose. Effect sizes range from approximately +4% to +20% higher dose per T allele in various studies, with meta-analyses estimating approximately +8-11% higher dose for T-allele carriers vs. CC.\n\n2. **Warfarin dose variability / Inter-individual variability in dose** - CYP4F2 rs2108622 accounts for approximately 1-7% of dose variance (after accounting for VKORC1 and CYP2C9), depending on the population studied.\n\n3. **Vitamin K1 (VK1) oxidation / hepatic VK1 metabolism** - The TT genotype is associated with reduced CYP4F2 protein concentration, reduced VK1 oxidase activity (~60% of wild-type), and elevated hepatic VK1 levels (PMC2684883).\n\n4. **Time to achieve therapeutic INR** - rs2108622 (via its LD partner rs2189784) was associated with altered time to reach therapeutic INR in one study (PMC5449482).\n\n5. **Stable warfarin dose attainment** - Carriers take longer or shorter time to achieve stable anticoagulation (PMC4803610).\n\n6. **Proportion of time in therapeutic range (PTTR)** - Studied in PMC4803610; CYP4F2*3 effects on PTTR examined.\n\n7. **Over-anticoagulation (INR >4)** - Studied in PMC4803610 and PMC4702374; CYP4F2*3/rs2108622 was examined for association with supratherapeutic INR. The *3/*3 genotype was associated with lower risk of overanticoagulation (recessive protective effect) in European Americans.\n\n8. **20-HETE (20-hydroxyeicosatetraenoic acid) production** - rs2108622 T allele reduces CYP4F2 enzyme activity and hence reduces 20-HETE biosynthesis (mentioned in PMC2288721).\n\n9. **CYP4F2 mRNA expression in liver** - rs2108622 T allele associated with increased CYP4F2 mRNA expression in liver (PMC5449482), while also being associated with decreased CYP4F11 mRNA expression.\n\n### Clinical Phenotypes / Conditions for Which Warfarin is Prescribed\n\nThese conditions appear as the underlying indications for warfarin therapy in the study populations studied:\n\n1. **Atrial fibrillation (AF)** - The most frequent indication; mentioned in PMC4441275, PMC4812555, PMC5006145, PMC2288721, PMC5610780, PMC2652833\n2. **Venous thromboembolism (VTE)** / **Deep vein thrombosis (DVT)** - PMC4441275, PMC4812555, PMC5006145, PMC5449482, PMC2652833\n3. **Pulmonary embolism (PE)** - PMC4812555, PMC5006145, PMC5610780, PMC2652833\n4. **Mechanical heart valve replacement (MHVR)** - Major indication in PMC2911553, PMC4134280, PMC4296935, PMC5006145\n5. **Stroke prevention** - PMC2652833, PMC5610780, PMC6510382, PMC2288721\n6. **Myocardial infarction** - PMC2992873 (WARIS-II trial: warfarin for secondary prevention post-MI)\n7. **Cardiovascular disease (general)** - PMC5610780 (Iranian cardiovascular patients), PMC2288721\n8. **Rheumatic heart disease (RHD)** - PMC5006145\n9. **Cardiac valve replacement** - PMC4296935, PMC4134280, PMC3098751\n10. **Hip or knee arthroplasty** - PMC5818817 (GIFT trial)\n11. **Heart disease in children** - PMC3384479, PMC4168390 (pediatric cardiac patients)\n    - Specific conditions: cardiac valve replacement, total cavopulmonary connection, dilated cardiomyopathy, Kawasaki disease coronary aneurysms\n12. **Thromboembolism prevention** - General indication mentioned in many papers\n13. **Coronary malfunction** - PMC2652833\n\n### Clinical Adverse Event Phenotypes\n\n1. **Hemorrhage / Bleeding** - PMC4803610 (major outcome: risk of major hemorrhage associated with CYP4F2*3; TT/homozygous variant shows protective pattern), PMC2288721, PMC5449482, PMC4812555, PMC5818817 (GIFT trial: major bleeding as composite primary outcome)\n2. **Over-anticoagulation / supratherapeutic INR** - PMC4803610, PMC4702374, PMC5818817\n3. **Thromboembolism / thrombotic events** (as adverse outcome of under-dosing) - PMC5818817, PMC2288721\n4. **Necrosis** (warfarin-related) - mentioned as a side effect of warfarin in PMC5610780\n5. **Osteoporosis** (warfarin-related) - mentioned as a side effect in PMC5610780\n6. **Purple toe syndrome** (warfarin-related) - mentioned as a side effect in PMC5610780\n\n### Metabolic/Enzymatic Phenotypes\n\n1. **Vitamin K metabolism** - CYP4F2 is a VK1 oxidase; rs2108622 T allele reduces VK1 catabolism (PMC2684883, PMC5449482)\n2. **Vitamin K1 (phylloquinone) oxidation activity** - Directly measured and reduced in TT vs. CC liver microsomes (PMC2684883)\n3. **CYP4F2 protein concentration in liver** - Reduced in T-allele carriers (PMC2684883)\n4. **20-HETE production** - Reduced by T allele via decreased CYP4F2 activity (PMC2288721, PMC2684883)\n\n### Phenotypes Studied But NOT Significantly Associated\n\nSeveral papers tested rs2108622 against phenotypes but found no significant association:\n\n- **Hypertension** - Despite the known role of 20-HETE in blood pressure regulation, hypertension was not directly studied as an outcome in these papers (though mentioned as context in PMC4134280)\n- **CYP4F2 mRNA expression** in some studies (PMC2684883 found no association between rs2108622 and mRNA in 59 liver samples, though protein was affected; PMC5449482 found the opposite in a different liver bank)\n- Warfarin dose in **Egyptian patients** (PMC3038469): not significant\n- Warfarin dose in **African Americans** (PMC4803610, PMC3586586): not significant\n- Warfarin dose in multiethnic diverse populations (PMC4441275): not significant in multiethnic cohort\n- Warfarin dose in **children** (PMC4168390): not significant\n- Warfarin dose in **Norwegian MI patients** (PMC2992873): weak/non-significant\n\n---\n\n## Paper-by-Paper Summary\n\n| PMC ID | Title/Focus | Drugs | rs2108622 Association |\n|---|---|---|---|\n| **PMC2288721** (Caldwell 2008) | Discovery paper: CYP4F2 variant alters required warfarin dose | Warfarin | T allele associated with ~1 mg/day higher dose; Carriers of the TT genotype require approximately 1 mg/day more warfarin than CC homozygotes; CT heterozygotes fall in between. The biological mechanism: CYP4F2 is a vitamin K1 (VK1) oxidase; the T allele leads to reduced hepatic CYP4F2 protein and enzyme activity, resulting in elevated hepatic VK1 levels, which in turn requires more warfarin to achieve the same anticoagulant effect.\n\n### Secondary Drugs (Coumarins/Vitamin K Antagonists)\n\nSeveral papers explicitly address other coumarin-class anticoagulants:\n\n- **Acenocoumarol** - explicitly included in meta-analyses (PMC6542461, PMC3731755). The CYP4F2*3 effect is reported to be **larger** in patients taking acenocoumarol than warfarin.\n- **Phenprocoumon** - included in the meta-analysis (PMC6542461); one cohort studied.\n- **Fluindione** - studied alongside warfarin in the pediatric VKA study (PMC3384479), though CYP4F2 was not significantly associated with fluindione dose in that study.\n- **Coumarins (generic)** - both meta-analyses (PMC3731755, PMC6542461) use \"coumarin\" as the umbrella term for all VKAs in the drug class.\n\n### Other Drugs Mentioned in Context (not directly associated with rs2108622 as the causal variant, but studied alongside it)\n\nThese appear in multi-drug pharmacogenetic studies testing CYP4F2 alongside other agents:\n- **Aspirin** (PMC2992873 - WARIS-II study: warfarin vs. aspirin vs. combination)\n- **Amiodarone** - repeatedly listed as a concomitant medication affecting warfarin dose in regression models (PMC2911553, PMC5508045, PMC5610780)\n- **Antiplatelet drugs** (PMC5508045)\n\n---\n\n## Phenotypes/Conditions Associated with rs2108622\n\n### Pharmacokinetic/Pharmacodynamic Phenotypes (Primary Outcomes)\n\n1. **Warfarin/coumarin maintenance dose requirement** - The principal phenotype across all 31 papers. The T allele is consistently associated with higher maintenance dose. Effect sizes range from approximately +4% to +20% higher dose per T allele in various studies, with meta-analyses estimating approximately +8-11% higher dose for T-allele carriers vs. CC.\n\n2. **Warfarin dose variability / Inter-individual variability in dose** - CYP4F2 rs2108622 accounts for approximately 1-7% of dose variance (after accounting for VKORC1 and CYP2C9), depending on the population studied.\n\n3. **Vitamin K1 (VK1) oxidation / hepatic VK1 metabolism** - The TT genotype is associated with reduced CYP4F2 protein concentration, reduced VK1 oxidase activity (~60% of wild-type), and elevated hepatic VK1 levels (PMC2684883).\n\n4. **Time to achieve therapeutic INR** - rs2108622 (via its LD partner rs2189784) was associated with altered time to reach therapeutic INR in one study (PMC5449482).\n\n5. **Stable warfarin dose attainment** - Carriers take longer or shorter time to achieve stable anticoagulation (PMC4803610).\n\n6. **Proportion of time in therapeutic range (PTTR)** - Studied in PMC4803610; CYP4F2*3 effects on PTTR examined.\n\n7. **Over-anticoagulation (INR >4)** - Studied in PMC4803610 and PMC4702374; CYP4F2*3/rs2108622 was examined for association with supratherapeutic INR. The *3/*3 genotype was associated with lower risk of overanticoagulation (recessive protective effect) in European Americans.\n\n8. **20-HETE (20-hydroxyeicosatetraenoic acid) production** - rs2108622 T allele reduces CYP4F2 enzyme activity and hence reduces 20-HETE biosynthesis (mentioned in PMC2288721).\n\n9. **CYP4F2 mRNA expression in liver** - rs2108622 T allele associated with increased CYP4F2 mRNA expression in liver (PMC5449482), while also being associated with decreased CYP4F11 mRNA expression.\n\n### Clinical Phenotypes / Conditions for Which Warfarin is Prescribed\n\nThese conditions appear as the underlying indications for warfarin therapy in the study populations studied:\n\n1. **Atrial fibrillation (AF)** - The most frequent indication; mentioned in PMC4441275, PMC4812555, PMC5006145, PMC2288721, PMC5610780, PMC2652833\n2. **Venous thromboembolism (VTE)** / **Deep vein thrombosis (DVT)** - PMC4441275, PMC4812555, PMC5006145, PMC5449482, PMC2652833\n3. **Pulmonary embolism (PE)** - PMC4812555, PMC5006145, PMC5610780, PMC2652833\n4. **Mechanical heart valve replacement (MHVR)** - Major indication in PMC2911553, PMC4134280, PMC4296935, PMC5006145\n5. **Stroke prevention** - PMC2652833, PMC5610780, PMC6510382, PMC2288721\n6. **Myocardial infarction** - PMC2992873 (WARIS-II trial: warfarin for secondary prevention post-MI)\n7. **Cardiovascular disease (general)** - PMC5610780 (Iranian cardiovascular patients), PMC2288721\n8. **Rheumatic heart disease (RHD)** - PMC5006145\n9. **Cardiac valve replacement** - PMC4296935, PMC4134280, PMC3098751\n10. **Hip or knee arthroplasty** - PMC5818817 (GIFT trial)\n11. **Heart disease in children** - PMC3384479, PMC4168390 (pediatric cardiac patients)\n    - Specific conditions: cardiac valve replacement, total cavopulmonary connection, dilated cardiomyopathy, Kawasaki disease coronary aneurysms\n12. **Thromboembolism prevention** - General indication mentioned in many papers\n13. **Coronary malfunction** - PMC2652833\n\n### Clinical Adverse Event Phenotypes\n\n1. **Hemorrhage / Bleeding** - PMC4803610 (major outcome: risk of major hemorrhage associated with CYP4F2*3; TT/homozygous variant shows protective pattern), PMC2288721, PMC5449482, PMC4812555, PMC5818817 (GIFT trial: major bleeding as composite primary outcome)\n2. **Over-anticoagulation / supratherapeutic INR** - PMC4803610, PMC4702374, PMC5818817\n3. **Thromboembolism / thrombotic events** (as adverse outcome of under-dosing) - PMC5818817, PMC2288721\n4. **Necrosis** (warfarin-related) - mentioned as a side effect of warfarin in PMC5610780\n5. **Osteoporosis** (warfarin-related) - mentioned as a side effect in PMC5610780\n6. **Purple toe syndrome** (warfarin-related) - mentioned as a side effect in PMC5610780\n\n### Metabolic/Enzymatic Phenotypes\n\n1. **Vitamin K metabolism** - CYP4F2 is a VK1 oxidase; rs2108622 T allele reduces VK1 catabolism (PMC2684883, PMC5449482)\n2. **Vitamin K1 (phylloquinone) oxidation activity** - Directly measured and reduced in TT vs. CC liver microsomes (PMC2684883)\n3. **CYP4F2 protein concentration in liver** - Reduced in T-allele carriers (PMC2684883)\n4. **20-HETE production** - Reduced by T allele via decreased CYP4F2 activity (PMC2288721, PMC2684883)\n\n### Phenotypes Studied But NOT Significantly Associated\n\nSeveral papers tested rs2108622 against phenotypes but found no significant association:\n\n- **Hypertension** - Despite the known role of 20-HETE in blood pressure regulation, hypertension was not directly studied as an outcome in these papers (though mentioned as context in PMC4134280)\n- **CYP4F2 mRNA expression** in some studies (PMC2684883 found no association between rs2108622 and mRNA in 59 liver samples, though protein was affected; PMC5449482 found the opposite in a different liver bank)\n- Warfarin dose in **Egyptian patients** (PMC3038469): not significant\n- Warfarin dose in **African Americans** (PMC4803610, PMC3586586): not significant\n- Warfarin dose in multiethnic diverse populations (PMC4441275): not significant in multiethnic cohort\n- Warfarin dose in **children** (PMC4168390): not significant\n- Warfarin dose in **Norwegian MI patients** (PMC2992873): weak/non-significant\n\n---\n\n## Paper-by-Paper Summary\n\n| PMC ID | Title/Focus | Drugs | rs2108622 Association |\n|---|---|---|---|\n| **PMC2288721** (Caldwell 2008) | Discovery paper: CYP4F2 variant alters required warfarin dose | Warfarin | T allele associated with ~1 mg/day higher dose; TT vs. CC; confirmed in 3 US Caucasian cohorts; linked to reduced 20-HETE production |\n| **PMC2652833** (Takeuchi 2009) | GWAS confirms VKORC1, CYP2C9, CYP4F2 as principal warfarin determinants | Warfarin | rs2108622 achieved genome-wide significance (p=8.3x10-10) for warfarin dose; effect of rs2108622 confirmed and larger than for warfarin)\n3. **Phenprocoumon** (vitamin K antagonist; one cohort in the large IPD meta-analysis)\n4. **Fluindione** (non-coumarin VKA; studied but not significantly associated in pediatric study)\n5. **Coumarins (class)** (generic term used in meta-analyses for the VKA drug class)\n\n### All Phenotypes/Conditions Associated with rs2108622\n\n**Pharmacological phenotypes (dose/response):**\n- Higher warfarin/coumarin maintenance dose requirement (T allele carriers require more drug)\n- Reduced risk of hemorrhage (CYP4F2*3/*3 homozygotes in European Americans; Neither the univariate nor the multivariate analysis identified genetic or nongenetic factors associated with the time spent within, above, or below the INR range.\n168-\n169-## Herein, we evaluate the influence of CYP4F2*3 on warfarin dose, time to target INR and stable dose, proportion of time spent in target range (PTTR), as well as the risk of overanticoagulation and hemorrhage among European and African Americans.\n    17→\n    18→**Design:** \n    19→CYP4F2*3 was genotyped in 1238 patients initiated on warfarin in a prospective inception cohort. Multivariable linear regression was used to assess warfarin dose and PTTR; proportional hazards analysis was performed to evaluate time to target INR and stable dose, overanticoagulation, and hemorrhage.\n    20→\n    21→**Setting:** \n    22→Two outpatient anticoagulation clinics\n    23→\n    24→**Participants:** \n    25→1238 anticoagulated patients\n    26→\n    27→**Outcomes:** \n    28→Warfarin dose (mg/day), time to target INR and stable dose, PTTR, over anticoagulation (INR>4), and major hemorrhage.\n    29→\n    30→**Results:** \n    31→Minor allele frequency for the CYP4F2*3 variant was 30.3% among European Americans and 8.4% among African Americans. CYP4F2*3 was associated with higher dose among European Americans but not African Americans. Compared to CYP4F2*1/*1, *1/*3 was associated with a statistically nonsignificant increase in dose (4.5%, p=0.22) and *3/*3 was associated with a statistically significant increase in dose (13.2% , p=0.02). CYP4F2 genotype did not influence time to target INR, time to stable dose, or PTTR in either race group. CYP4F2*3/*3 was associated with a 31% lower risk of over anticoagulation (p=0.06). Incidence of hemorrhage was lower among participants with CYP4F2 *3/*3 compared to *1/*3 or *1/*1 (IRR=0.45; 95% CI: 0.14 – 1.11; p=0.09). After controlling for covariates, the CYP4F2 *3/*3 was associated with a 52% lower risk of hemorrhage although this was not statistically significant (p=0.24).\n    32→\n    33→**Conclusion:** \n    34→Possession of CYP4F2*3 variant influences warfarin dose among European Americans but not African Americans. The CYP4F2-dose, CYP4F2-overanticoagulation, and CYP4F2-hemorrhage association follows a recessive pattern with possession of CYP4F2*3/*3 genotype likely demonstrating a protective effect. These findings need further confirmation.\n    35→\n    36→Keywords: CYP4F2, race, warfarin dose, PTTR, over anticoagulation, hemorrhage\n    37→\n    38→### Objective\n    39→\n    40→The p.V433M in cytochrome P450 4F2 (rs2108622, *CYP4F2*3*) is associated with higher warfarin dose and lower risk of hemorrhage among European Americans. Herein, we evaluate the influence of CYP4F2*3 on warfarin dose, time to target INR and stable dose, proportion of time spent in target range (PTTR), as well as the risk of overanticoagulation and hemorrhage among European and African Americans.\n\n**Design:** \nCYP4F2*3 was genotyped in 1238 patients initiated on warfarin in a prospective inception cohort. Multivariable linear regression was used to assess warfarin dose and PTTR; proportional hazards analysis was performed to evaluate time to target INR and stable dose, overanticoagulation, and hemorrhage.\n\n**Setting:** \nTwo outpatient anticoagulation clinics\n\n**Participants:** \n1238 anticoagulated patients\n\n**Outcomes:** \nWarfarin dose (mg/day), time to target INR and stable dose, PTTR, over anticoagulation (INR>4), and major hemorrhage.\n\n**Results:** \nMinor allele frequency for the CYP4F2*3 variant was 30.3% among European Americans and 8.4% among African Americans. CYP4F2*3 was associated with higher dose among European Americans but not African Americans. Compared to CYP4F2*1/*1, *1/*3 was associated with a statistically nonsignificant increase in dose (4.5%, p=0.22) and *3/*3 was associated with a statistically significant increase in dose (13.2% , p=0.02). CYP4F2 genotype did not influence time to target INR, time to stable dose, or PTTR in either race group. CYP4F2*3/*3 was associated with a 31% lower risk of over anticoagulation (p=0.06). Incidence of hemorrhage was lower among participants with CYP4F2 *3/*3 compared to *1/*3 or *1/*1 (IRR=0.45; 95% CI: 0.14 – 1.11; p=0.09). After controlling for covariates, the CYP4F2 *3/*3 was associated with a 52% lower risk of hemorrhage although this was not statistically significant (p=0.24).\n\n**Conclusion:** \nPossession of CYP4F2*3 variant influences warfarin dose among European Americans but not African Americans. The CYP4F2-dose, CYP4F2-overanticoagulation, and CYP4F2-hemorrhage association follows a recessive pattern with possession of CYP4F2*3/*3 genotype likely demonstrating a protective effect. These findings need further confirmation.\n\nKeywords: CYP4F2, race, warfarin dose, PTTR, over anticoagulation, hemorrhage\n\n### Objective\n\nThe p.V433M in cytochrome P450 4F2 (rs2108622, *CYP4F2*3*) is associated with higher warfarin dose and lower risk of hemorrhage among European Americans.
